					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Form of shared internet-based computing</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Cloud Computing" redirects here; not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Cloud_Computing_(horse)" title="Cloud Computing (horse)">Cloud Computing (horse)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cloud_computing.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Cloud_computing.svg/220px-Cloud_computing.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="199" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Cloud_computing.svg/330px-Cloud_computing.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Cloud_computing.svg/440px-Cloud_computing.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1175" data-file-height="1064" /></a><figcaption>Cloud computing metaphor: the group of networked elements providing services does not need to be addressed or managed individually by users; instead, the entire provider-managed suite of hardware and software can be thought of as an amorphous cloud.</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Cloud computing</b> is "a paradigm for enabling network access to a scalable and elastic pool of shareable physical or virtual resources with self-service provisioning and administration on-demand," according to <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Essential_Characteristics">Essential Characteristics</h2></div>
<p>In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST) identified five "essential characteristics" for cloud systems.<sup id="cite_ref-nist_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nist-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Below are the exact definitions according to NIST:<sup id="cite_ref-nist_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nist-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>On-demand self-service: "A consumer can unilaterally provision computing capabilities, such as server time and network storage, as needed automatically without requiring human interaction with each service provider."</li>
<li>Broad network access: "Capabilities are available over the network and accessed through standard mechanisms that promote use by heterogeneous thin or thick client platforms (e.g., mobile phones, tablets, <a href="/wiki/Laptop" title="Laptop">laptops</a>, and workstations)."</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pooling_(resource_management)" title="Pooling (resource management)">Resource pooling</a>: " The provider's computing resources are pooled to serve multiple consumers using a multi-tenant model, with different physical and virtual resources dynamically assigned and reassigned according to consumer demand."</li>
<li>Rapid elasticity: "Capabilities can be elastically provisioned and released, in some cases automatically, to scale rapidly outward and inward commensurate with demand. To the consumer, the capabilities available for provisioning often appear unlimited and can be appropriated in any quantity at any time."</li>
<li>Measured service: "Cloud systems automatically control and optimize resource use by leveraging a metering capability at some level of abstraction appropriate to the type of service (e.g., storage, processing, bandwidth, and active user accounts). Resource usage can be monitored, controlled, and reported, providing transparency for both the provider and consumer of the utilized service.</li></ul>
<p>By 2023, the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a> (ISO) had expanded and refined the list.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_cloud_computing" title="History of cloud computing">History of cloud computing</a></div>
<p>The history of cloud computing extends back to the 1960s, with the initial concepts of time-sharing becoming popularized via <a href="/wiki/Remote_job_entry" title="Remote job entry">remote job entry</a> (RJE). The "data center" model, where users submitted jobs to operators to run on mainframes, was predominantly used during this era. This was a time of exploration and experimentation with ways to make large-scale computing power available to more users through <a href="/wiki/Time-sharing" title="Time-sharing">time-sharing</a>, optimizing the infrastructure, platform, and applications, and increasing efficiency for end users.<sup id="cite_ref-rfc105_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc105-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The "cloud" metaphor for virtualized services dates to 1994, when it was used by <a href="/wiki/General_Magic" title="General Magic">General Magic</a> for the universe of "places" that mobile agents in the <a href="/wiki/Telescript_(programming_language)" title="Telescript (programming language)">Telescript</a> environment could "go". The metaphor is credited to David Hoffman, a General Magic communications specialist, based on its long-standing use in networking and telecom.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The expression <i>cloud computing</i> became more widely known in 1996 when <a href="/wiki/Compaq_Computer_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Compaq Computer Corporation">Compaq Computer Corporation</a> drew up a <a href="/wiki/Business_plan" title="Business plan">business plan</a> for future computing and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>. The company's ambition was to supercharge <a href="/wiki/Sales" title="Sales">sales</a> with "cloud computing-enabled applications". The business plan foresaw that online consumer file storage would likely be commercially successful. As a result, Compaq decided to sell <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">server</a> hardware to <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">internet service providers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2000s, the application of cloud computing began to take shape with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Web_Services" title="Amazon Web Services">Amazon Web Services</a> (AWS) in 2002, which allowed developers to build applications independently. In 2006 Amazon Simple Storage Service, known as <a href="/wiki/Amazon_S3" title="Amazon S3">Amazon S3</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Elastic_Compute_Cloud" title="Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud">Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud</a> (EC2) were released. In 2008 <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s development of the first <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open-source software</a> for deploying private and hybrid clouds.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The following decade saw the launch of various cloud services. In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> launched <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Azure" title="Microsoft Azure">Microsoft Azure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rackspace_Technology" title="Rackspace Technology">Rackspace Hosting</a> and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> initiated an open-source cloud-software project, <a href="/wiki/OpenStack" title="OpenStack">OpenStack</a>. <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/IBM_Cloud" title="IBM Cloud">IBM SmartCloud</a> framework in 2011, and <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Corporation" title="Oracle Corporation">Oracle</a> announced the <a href="/wiki/Oracle_Cloud" title="Oracle Cloud">Oracle Cloud</a> in 2012. In December 2019, Amazon launched AWS Outposts, a service that extends AWS infrastructure, services, <a href="/wiki/API" title="API">APIs</a>, and tools to customer data centers, co-location spaces, or on-premises facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Azure_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Azure-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Value_proposition">Value proposition</h2></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1251242444">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .ambox{margin:0 10%}}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .ambox{display:none!important}}</style><table class="box-Page_numbers_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Text_document_with_page_number_icon.svg/40px-Text_document_with_page_number_icon.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Text_document_with_page_number_icon.svg/60px-Text_document_with_page_number_icon.svg.png 1.5x, /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section cites its <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">sources</a> but <b>does not provide <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITEHOW" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITEHOW">page references</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You can help by providing page numbers for existing
